     <p>
      The
      <b>
       euro
      </b>
      (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Currency_sign" title="Currency sign">
       sign
      </a>
      :
      <b>
       <a href="/wiki/Euro_sign" title="Euro sign">
        €
       </a>
      </b>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/ISO_4217" title="ISO 4217">
       code
      </a>
      :
      <b>
       EUR
      </b>
      ) is the official currency of the
      <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">
       eurozone
      </a>
      , which consists of 19 of the 28
      <a href="/wiki/Member_state_of_the_European_Union" title="Member state of the European Union">
       member states of the European Union
      </a>
      :
      <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">
       Austria
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">
       Belgium
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">
       Cyprus
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">
       Estonia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">
       Finland
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">
       France
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">
       Germany
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">
       Greece
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">
       Ireland
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
       Italy
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">
       Latvia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">
       Lithuania
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">
       Luxembourg
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">
       Malta
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">
       Netherlands
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">
       Portugal
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">
       Slovakia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">
       Slovenia
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">
       Spain
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-About_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-About-19">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The currency is also officially used by the
      <a href="/wiki/Institutions_of_the_European_Union" title="Institutions of the European Union">
       institutions of the European Union
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/International_status_and_usage_of_the_euro" title="International status and usage of the euro">
       four other European countries
      </a>
      , as well as unilaterally by two others, and is consequently used daily by some 337 million
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Europeans" title="Europeans">
       Europeans
      </a>
      as of 2015
      <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
       <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit">
        [update]
       </a>
      </sup>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Outside of Europe, a number of
      <a href="/wiki/Special_member_state_territories_and_the_European_Union" title="Special member state territories and the European Union">
       overseas territories of EU members
      </a>
      also use the euro as their currency.
     </p>
     <p>
      Additionally, 210 million people worldwide as of 2013
      <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
       <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit">
        [update]
       </a>
      </sup>
      use currencies
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fixed_exchange_rate" title="Fixed exchange rate">
       pegged
      </a>
      to the euro. The euro is the second largest
      <a href="/wiki/Reserve_currency" title="Reserve currency">
       reserve currency
      </a>
      as well as the second most traded currency in the world after the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">
       United States dollar
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As of August 2014
      <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
       <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit">
        [update]
       </a>
      </sup>
      , with more than €995,000,000,000 in circulation, the euro has the highest combined value of banknotes and coins in
      <a href="/wiki/Circulation_(currency)" title="Circulation (currency)">
       circulation
      </a>
      in the world, having surpassed the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">
       U.S. dollar
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        [note 17]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers">
         <span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (July 2015)">
          needs update
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Based on
      <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">
       International Monetary Fund
      </a>
      estimates of 2008
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GDP" title="GDP">
       GDP
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">
       purchasing power parity
      </a>
      among the various currencies, the eurozone is the second largest economy in the world.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The name
      <i>
       euro
      </i>
      was officially adopted on 16 December 1995.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-madrid1995_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-madrid1995-27">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The euro was introduced to world financial markets as an
      <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_account" title="Unit of account">
       accounting currency
      </a>
      on 1 January 1999, replacing the former
      <a href="/wiki/European_Currency_Unit" title="European Currency Unit">
       European Currency Unit
      </a>
      (ECU) at a ratio of 1:1 (US$1.1743). Physical euro
      <a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">
       coins
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Banknote" title="Banknote">
       banknotes
      </a>
      entered into circulation on 1 January 2002, making it the day-to-day operating currency of its original members, and by May 2002 had completely replaced the former currencies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
      While the euro dropped subsequently to US$0.8252 within two years (26 October 2000), it has traded above the U.S. dollar since the end of 2002, peaking at US$1.6038 on 18 July 2008.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Since late 2009, the euro has been immersed in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_sovereign-debt_crisis" title="European sovereign-debt crisis">
       European sovereign-debt crisis
      </a>
      which has led to the creation of the
      <a href="/wiki/European_Financial_Stability_Facility" title="European Financial Stability Facility">
       European Financial Stability Facility
      </a>
      as well as
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_euro#Reform" title="History of the euro">
       other reforms
      </a>
      aimed at stabilising the currency. In July 2012, the euro fell below US$1.21 for the first time in two years, following concerns raised over Greek debt and Spain's troubled banking sector.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As of 28 October 2016
      <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
       <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit">
        [update]
       </a>
      </sup>
      , the euro–dollar exchange rate stands at ~ US$1.0922.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Administration">
       Administration
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Administration">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6000" data-file-width="3622" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg/150px-European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg/225px-European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg/300px-European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/European_Central_Bank" title="European Central Bank">
         European Central Bank
        </a>
        has
        <a href="/wiki/Seat_of_the_European_Central_Bank" title="Seat of the European Central Bank">
         its seat
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">
         Frankfurt
        </a>
        (Germany) and is in charge of the monetary policy of the
        <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">
         euro area
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/European_Central_Bank" title="European Central Bank">
       European Central Bank
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Maastricht_Treaty" title="Maastricht Treaty">
       Maastricht Treaty
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Eurogroup" title="Eurogroup">
       Eurogroup
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The euro is managed and administered by the
      <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">
       Frankfurt
      </a>
      -based
      <a href="/wiki/European_Central_Bank" title="European Central Bank">
       European Central Bank
      </a>
      (ECB) and the
      <a href="/wiki/Eurosystem" title="Eurosystem">
       Eurosystem
      </a>
      (composed of the
      <a href="/wiki/Central_bank" title="Central bank">
       central banks
      </a>
      of the eurozone countries). As an independent central bank, the ECB has sole authority to set
      <a href="/wiki/Monetary_policy" title="Monetary policy">
       monetary policy
      </a>
      . The Eurosystem participates in the printing, minting and distribution of
      <a href="/wiki/Euro_banknotes" title="Euro banknotes">
       notes
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Euro_coins" title="Euro coins">
       coins
      </a>
      in all member states, and the operation of the eurozone payment systems.
     </p>
     <p>
      The 1992
      <a href="/wiki/Maastricht_Treaty" title="Maastricht Treaty">
       Maastricht Treaty
      </a>
      obliges most EU member states to adopt the euro upon meeting certain monetary and budgetary
      <a href="/wiki/Euro_convergence_criteria" title="Euro convergence criteria">
       convergence criteria
      </a>
      , although not all states have done so. The United Kingdom and Denmark negotiated exemptions,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
      while Sweden (which joined the EU in 1995, after the Maastricht Treaty was signed) turned down the euro in a 2003 referendum, and has circumvented the obligation to adopt the euro by not meeting the monetary and budgetary requirements. All nations that have joined the EU since 1993 have pledged to adopt the euro in due course.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Issuing_modalities_for_banknotes">
       Issuing modalities for banknotes
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Issuing modalities for banknotes">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Since 5 January 2002, the national central banks (NCBs) and the
      <a href="/wiki/European_Central_Bank" title="European Central Bank">
       ECB
      </a>
      have issued euro banknotes on a joint basis.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scheller_33-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Scheller-33">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Euro banknotes do not show which central bank issued them. Eurosystem NCBs are required to accept euro banknotes put into circulation by other Eurosystem members and these banknotes are not repatriated. The ECB issues 8% of the total value of banknotes issued by the Eurosystem.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scheller_33-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Scheller-33">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In practice, the ECB's banknotes are put into circulation by the NCBs, thereby incurring matching liabilities vis-à-vis the ECB. These liabilities carry interest at the main refinancing rate of the ECB. The other 92% of the euro banknotes are issued by the NCBs in proportion to their respective shares in the capital key of the ECB,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scheller_33-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Scheller-33">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      calculated using national share of European Union population and national share of European Union GDP, equally weighted.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">
       Characteristics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Characteristics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Coins_and_banknotes">
       Coins and banknotes
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Coins and banknotes">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Euro_coins" title="Euro coins">
       Euro coins
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Euro_banknotes" title="Euro banknotes">
       Euro banknotes
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Euro_coins_and_banknotes.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Euro_coins_and_banknotes.jpg/220px-Euro_coins_and_banknotes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Euro_coins_and_banknotes.jpg/330px-Euro_coins_and_banknotes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Euro_coins_and_banknotes.jpg/440px-Euro_coins_and_banknotes.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Euro_coins_and_banknotes.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Euro coins and banknotes of various denominations.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The euro is divided into 100
      <a href="/wiki/Cent_(currency)" title="Cent (currency)">
       cents
      </a>
      (sometimes referred to as
      <i>
       euro cents
      </i>
      , especially when distinguishing them from other currencies, and referred to as such on the common side of all cent coins). In Community legislative acts the plural forms of
      <i>
       euro
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       cent
      </i>
      are spelled without the
      <i>
       s
      </i>
      , notwithstanding normal English usage.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ec.europa.eu_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ec.europa.eu-35">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Otherwise, normal English plurals are sometimes used,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
      with many
      <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_issues_concerning_the_euro" title="Linguistic issues concerning the euro">
       local variations
      </a>
      such as
      <i>
       centime
      </i>
      in France.
     </p>
     <p>
      All circulating coins have a
      <i>
       common side
      </i>
      showing the denomination or value, and a map in the background. Due to the
      <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_European_Union" title="Languages of the European Union">
       linguistic plurality of Europe
      </a>
      , the Latin alphabet version of
      <i>
       euro
      </i>
      is used (as opposed to the less common Greek or Cyrillic) and
      <a href="/wiki/Arabic_numerals" title="Arabic numerals">
       Arabic numerals
      </a>
      (other text is used on national sides in national languages, but other text on the common side is avoided). For the denominations except the 1-, 2- and 5-cent coins, the map only showed the 15 member states which were members when the euro was introduced. Beginning in 2007 or 2008 (depending on the country) the old map is being replaced by a map of Europe also showing countries outside the Union like Norway. The 1-, 2- and 5-cent coins, however, keep their old design, showing a geographical map of Europe with the 15 member states of 2002 raised somewhat above the rest of the map. All common sides were designed by
      <a href="/wiki/Luc_Luycx" title="Luc Luycx">
       Luc Luycx
      </a>
      . The coins also have a
      <i>
       national side
      </i>
      showing an image specifically chosen by the country that issued the coin. Euro coins from any member state may be freely used in any nation that has adopted the euro.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_finnish_2013_5_euro.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1040" data-file-width="1024" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/New_finnish_2013_5_euro.png/220px-New_finnish_2013_5_euro.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/New_finnish_2013_5_euro.png/330px-New_finnish_2013_5_euro.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/New_finnish_2013_5_euro.png/440px-New_finnish_2013_5_euro.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_finnish_2013_5_euro.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The new banknotes were introduced in the beginning of 2013. The top half of the image shows the front side of the banknote and the bottom half shows the back side.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EUR_10_obverse_(2014_issue).png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="310" data-file-width="596" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/EUR_10_obverse_%282014_issue%29.png/220px-EUR_10_obverse_%282014_issue%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/EUR_10_obverse_%282014_issue%29.png/330px-EUR_10_obverse_%282014_issue%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/EUR_10_obverse_%282014_issue%29.png/440px-EUR_10_obverse_%282014_issue%29.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EUR_10_obverse_(2014_issue).png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/10_euro_note" title="10 euro note">
         10 euro note
        </a>
        from the new Europa series written in
        <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">
         Latin
        </a>
        (EURO) and
        <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">
         Greek
        </a>
        (EYPΩ) alphabets, but also in the
        <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_alphabet" title="Bulgarian alphabet">
         Cyrillic
        </a>
        (ЕВРО) alphabet, as a result of
        <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">
         Bulgaria
        </a>
        joining the European Union in 2007.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The coins are issued in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2_euro_coins" title="2 euro coins">
       €2
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1_euro_coins" title="1 euro coins">
       €1
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/50_cent_euro_coins" title="50 cent euro coins">
       50c
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/20_cent_euro_coins" title="20 cent euro coins">
       20c
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/10_cent_euro_coins" title="10 cent euro coins">
       10c
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5_cent_euro_coins" title="5 cent euro coins">
       5c
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2_cent_euro_coins" title="2 cent euro coins">
       2c
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1_cent_euro_coins" title="1 cent euro coins">
       1c
      </a>
      denominations. To avoid the use of the two smallest coins, some cash transactions are rounded to the nearest five cents in the Netherlands and Ireland
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      (by voluntary agreement) and in Finland (by law).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This practice is discouraged by the Commission, as is the practice of certain shops to refuse to accept high value euro notes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%E2%82%AC2_commemorative_coins" title="€2 commemorative coins">
       Commemorative coins
      </a>
      with €2 face value have been issued with changes to the design of the national side of the coin. These include both commonly issued coins, such as the €2 commemorative coin for the fiftieth anniversary of the signing of the Treaty of Rome, and nationally issued coins, such as the coin to commemorate the
      <a href="/wiki/2004_Summer_Olympics" title="2004 Summer Olympics">
       2004 Summer Olympics
      </a>
      issued by Greece. These coins are legal tender throughout the eurozone. Collector coins with various other denominations have been issued as well, but these are not intended for general circulation, and they are legal tender only in the member state that issued them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The design for the
      <a href="/wiki/Euro_banknotes" title="Euro banknotes">
       euro banknotes
      </a>
      has common designs on both sides. The design was created by the Austrian designer
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Kalina" title="Robert Kalina">
       Robert Kalina
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euro96_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-euro96-43">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Notes are issued in
      <a href="/wiki/500_euro_note" title="500 euro note">
       €500
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/200_euro_note" title="200 euro note">
       €200
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/100_euro_note" title="100 euro note">
       €100
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/50_euro_note" title="50 euro note">
       €50
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/20_euro_note" title="20 euro note">
       €20
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/10_euro_note" title="10 euro note">
       €10
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/5_euro_note" title="5 euro note">
       €5
      </a>
      . Each banknote has its own colour and is dedicated to an artistic period of European architecture. The front of the note features windows or gateways while the back has bridges, symbolising links between countries and with the future. While the designs are supposed to be devoid of any identifiable characteristics, the initial designs by
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Kalina" title="Robert Kalina">
       Robert Kalina
      </a>
      were of specific bridges, including the
      <a href="/wiki/Rialto" title="Rialto">
       Rialto
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Pont_de_Neuilly" title="Pont de Neuilly">
       Pont de Neuilly
      </a>
      , and were subsequently rendered more generic; the final designs still bear very close similarities to their specific prototypes; thus they are not truly generic. The monuments looked similar enough to different national monuments to please everyone.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Payments_clearing.2C_electronic_funds_transfer">
       Payments clearing, electronic funds transfer
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Payments clearing, electronic funds transfer">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Single_Euro_Payments_Area" title="Single Euro Payments Area">
       Single Euro Payments Area
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Capital within the EU may be transferred in any amount from one country to another. All intra-EU transfers in euro are treated as domestic transactions and bear the corresponding domestic transfer costs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This includes all member states of the EU, even those outside the eurozone providing the transactions are carried out in euro.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Credit/debit card charging and ATM withdrawals within the eurozone are also treated as domestic transactions; however paper-based payment orders, like cheques, have not been standardised so these are still domestic-based. The ECB has also set up a
      <a href="/wiki/Clearing_(finance)" title="Clearing (finance)">
       clearing system
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TARGET" title="TARGET">
       TARGET
      </a>
      , for large euro transactions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Currency_sign">
       Currency sign
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Currency sign">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Euro_logo_plus_character.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="301" data-file-width="600" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Euro_logo_plus_character.png/220px-Euro_logo_plus_character.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Euro_logo_plus_character.png/330px-Euro_logo_plus_character.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Euro_logo_plus_character.png/440px-Euro_logo_plus_character.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Euro_logo_plus_character.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The euro sign; logotype and handwritten
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Euro_sign" title="Euro sign">
       Euro sign
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      A special
      <a href="/wiki/Euro_sign" title="Euro sign">
       euro currency sign
      </a>
      (€) was designed after a public survey had narrowed the original ten proposals down to two. The European Commission then chose the design created by the Belgian Alain Billiet.
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       Inspiration for the € symbol itself came from the
       <a href="/wiki/Epsilon" title="Epsilon">
        Greek epsilon
       </a>
       (Є)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [note 18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       – a reference to the cradle of European civilisation – and the first letter of the word Europe, crossed by two parallel lines to 'certify' the stability of the euro.
      </p>
      <div class="templatequotecite">
       <cite>
        —
        <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">
         European Commission
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ec.europa.eu_35-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-ec.europa.eu-35">
          [18]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </cite>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      The European Commission also specified a euro logo with exact proportions and foreground and background colour tones.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
      While the Commission intended the logo to be a prescribed glyph shape, font designers made it clear that they intended to design their own variants instead.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Typewriter" title="Typewriter">
       Typewriters
      </a>
      lacking the euro sign can create it by typing a capital 'C', backspacing and
      <a href="/wiki/Overstrike" title="Overstrike">
       overstriking
      </a>
      it with the equal ('=') sign. Placement of the currency sign relative to the numeric amount varies from nation to nation, but for texts in English the symbol (or the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">
       ISO
      </a>
      -standard "EUR") should precede the amount.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      There is no official symbol for the cent.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2015)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: History">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_euro" title="History of the euro">
       History of the euro
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction">

      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">

       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">

       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>

     <p>
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Template:Https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkish_lira&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Template:Https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkish lira (page does not exist)">
       Template:Https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkish lira
      </a>
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Template:Https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bulgarian_lev&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Template:Https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bulgarian lev (page does not exist)">
       Template:Https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bulgarian lev
      </a>
     </p>
     <p>
      The euro was established by the provisions in the 1992
      <a href="/wiki/Maastricht_Treaty" title="Maastricht Treaty">
       Maastricht Treaty
      </a>
      . To participate in the currency, member states are meant to meet
      <a href="/wiki/Euro_convergence_criteria" title="Euro convergence criteria">
       strict criteria
      </a>
      , such as a budget
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government_budget_deficit" title="Government budget deficit">
       deficit
      </a>
      of less than three percent of their GDP, a debt ratio of less than sixty percent of GDP (both of which were ultimately widely flouted after introduction), low inflation, and
      <a href="/wiki/Interest" title="Interest">
       interest
      </a>
      rates close to the EU average. In the Maastricht Treaty, the United Kingdom and Denmark were granted exemptions per their request from moving to the stage of monetary union which would result in the introduction of the euro.
     </p>
     <p>
      Economists who helped create or contributed to the euro include
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Fred_Arditti&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Fred Arditti (page does not exist)">
       Fred Arditti
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Neil_Dowling&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Neil Dowling (page does not exist)">
       Neil Dowling
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Wim_Duisenberg" title="Wim Duisenberg">
       Wim Duisenberg
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mundell" title="Robert Mundell">
       Robert Mundell
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Tommaso_Padoa-Schioppa" title="Tommaso Padoa-Schioppa">
       Tommaso Padoa-Schioppa
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Tollison" title="Robert Tollison">
       Robert Tollison
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="What are the criteria for inclusion in this list (December 2009)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      (For
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macroeconomic" title="Macroeconomic">
       macroeconomic
      </a>
      theory, see
      <a href="#Optimal_currency_area">
       below
      </a>
      .)
     </p>
     <p>
      The name "euro" was officially adopted in
      <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">
       Madrid
      </a>
      on 16 December 1995.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-madrid1995_27-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-madrid1995-27">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Belgian
      <a href="/wiki/Esperantist" title="Esperantist">
       Esperantist
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Germain_Pirlot" title="Germain Pirlot">
       Germain Pirlot
      </a>
      , a former teacher of French and history is credited with naming the new currency by sending a letter to then
      <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_European_Commission" title="President of the European Commission">
       President of the European Commission
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Santer" title="Jacques Santer">
       Jacques Santer
      </a>
      , suggesting the name "euro" on 4 August 1995.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Due to differences in national conventions for
      <a href="/wiki/Rounding" title="Rounding">
       rounding
      </a>
      and significant digits, all conversion between the national currencies had to be carried out using the process of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triangulation_(finance)" title="Triangulation (finance)">
       triangulation
      </a>
      via the euro. The
      <i>
       definitive
      </i>
      values of one euro in terms of the
      <a href="/wiki/Exchange_rate" title="Exchange rate">
       exchange rates
      </a>
      at which the currency entered the euro are shown on the right.
     </p>
     <p>
      The rates were determined by the
      <a href="/wiki/Council_of_the_European_Union" title="Council of the European Union">
       Council of the European Union
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        [note 19]
       </a>
      </sup>
      based on a recommendation from the European Commission based on the market rates on 31 December 1998. They were set so that one
      <a href="/wiki/European_Currency_Unit" title="European Currency Unit">
       European Currency Unit
      </a>
      (ECU) would equal one euro. The European Currency Unit was an accounting unit used by the EU, based on the currencies of the member states; it was not a currency in its own right. They could not be set earlier, because the ECU depended on the closing exchange rate of the non-euro currencies (principally the
      <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">
       pound sterling
      </a>
      ) that day.
     </p>
     <p>
      The procedure used to fix the conversion rate between the
      <a href="/wiki/Greek_drachma" title="Greek drachma">
       Greek drachma
      </a>
      and the euro was different, since the euro by then was already two years old. While the conversion rates for the initial eleven currencies were determined only hours before the euro was introduced, the conversion rate for the Greek drachma was fixed several months beforehand.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        [note 20]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The currency was introduced in non-physical form (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traveller%27s_cheque" title="Traveller's cheque">
       traveller's cheques
      </a>
      , electronic transfers, banking, etc.) at midnight on 1 January 1999, when the national currencies of participating countries (the eurozone) ceased to exist independently. Their exchange rates were locked at fixed rates against each other. The euro thus became the successor to the
      <a href="/wiki/European_Currency_Unit" title="European Currency Unit">
       European Currency Unit
      </a>
      (ECU). The notes and coins for the old currencies, however, continued to be used as
      <a href="/wiki/Legal_tender" title="Legal tender">
       legal tender
      </a>
      until new euro notes and coins were introduced on 1 January 2002.
     </p>
     <p>
      The changeover period during which the former currencies' notes and coins were exchanged for those of the euro lasted about two months, until 28 February 2002. The official date on which the national currencies ceased to be legal tender varied from member state to member state. The earliest date was in Germany, where the
      <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Mark" title="Deutsche Mark">
       mark
      </a>
      officially ceased to be legal tender on 31 December 2001, though the exchange period lasted for two months more. Even after the old currencies ceased to be legal tender, they continued to be accepted by national central banks for periods ranging from several years to forever (the latter in Austria, Germany, Ireland, Estonia and Latvia for banknotes and coins; also, Belgium, Luxembourg, Slovenia and Slovakia will accept banknotes forever, but not coins). The earliest coins to become non-convertible were the Portuguese
      <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_escudo" title="Portuguese escudo">
       escudos
      </a>
      , which ceased to have monetary value after 31 December 2002, although banknotes remain exchangeable until 2022.
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Eurozone_crisis">
       Eurozone crisis
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Eurozone crisis">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eurozone_crisis" title="Eurozone crisis">
       Eurozone crisis
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Greek_government-debt_crisis" title="Greek government-debt crisis">
       Greek government-debt crisis
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Government_surplus_or_deficit_(EU-USA-UK).png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="367" data-file-width="429" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Government_surplus_or_deficit_%28EU-USA-UK%29.png/220px-Government_surplus_or_deficit_%28EU-USA-UK%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Government_surplus_or_deficit_%28EU-USA-UK%29.png/330px-Government_surplus_or_deficit_%28EU-USA-UK%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0e/Government_surplus_or_deficit_%28EU-USA-UK%29.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Government_surplus_or_deficit_(EU-USA-UK).png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Budget deficit of the euro area compared to the United States and the UK.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Following the U.S. financial crisis in 2008, fears of a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sovereign_debt_crisis" title="Sovereign debt crisis">
       sovereign debt crisis
      </a>
      developed in 2009 among fiscally conservative investors concerning some European states, with the situation becoming particularly tense in
      <a href="/wiki/2000s_European_sovereign_debt_crisis_timeline" title="2000s European sovereign debt crisis timeline">
       early 2010
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This included eurozone members
      <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Greece" title="Economy of Greece">
       Greece
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj_58-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-wsj-58">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Economy of the Republic of Ireland">
       Ireland
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Portugal" title="Economy of Portugal">
       Portugal
      </a>
      and also some EU countries outside the area.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Iceland, the country which experienced the largest
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%9311_Icelandic_financial_crisis" title="2008–11 Icelandic financial crisis">
       crisis in 2008
      </a>
      when its entire international banking system collapsed, has emerged less affected by the sovereign-debt crisis as the government was unable to bail the banks out. In the EU, especially in countries where sovereign debts have increased sharply due to bank bailouts, a crisis of confidence has emerged with the widening of
      <a href="/wiki/Bond_(finance)" title="Bond (finance)">
       bond
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Yield_spread" title="Yield spread">
       yield spreads
      </a>
      and risk insurance on
      <a href="/wiki/Credit_default_swap" title="Credit default swap">
       credit default swaps
      </a>
      between these countries and other EU members, most importantly Germany.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
      To be included in the eurozone, the countries had to fulfil certain
      <a href="/wiki/Euro_convergence_criteria" title="Euro convergence criteria">
       convergence criteria
      </a>
      , but the meaningfulness of such criteria was diminished by the fact it was not enforced with the same degree of strictness from country to country.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        [42]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      According to the
      <a href="/wiki/Economist_Intelligence_Unit" title="Economist Intelligence Unit">
       Economist Intelligence Unit
      </a>
      in 2011, "[I]f the [euro area] is treated as a single entity, its [economic and fiscal] position looks no worse and in some respects, rather better than that of the US or the UK" and the budget deficit for the euro area as a whole is much lower and the euro area's government debt/GDP ratio of 86% in 2010 was about the same level as that of the United States. "Moreover", they write, "private-sector indebtedness across the euro area as a whole is markedly lower than in the highly leveraged
      <a href="/wiki/Anglosphere" title="Anglosphere">
       Anglo-Saxon
      </a>
      economies". The authors conclude that the crisis "is as much political as economic" and the result of the fact that the euro area lacks the support of "institutional
      <a href="/wiki/Paraphernalia" title="Paraphernalia">
       paraphernalia
      </a>
      (and mutual bonds of solidarity) of a state".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The crisis continued with S&amp;P downgrading nine euro-area countries, including France, then downgrading the entire
      <a href="/wiki/European_Financial_Stability_Facility" title="European Financial Stability Facility">
       European Financial Stability Facility
      </a>
      (EFSF) fund.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2012)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In May 2012, socialist
      <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Hollande" title="François Hollande">
       François Hollande
      </a>
      was elected as president of France and a month later the French socialist legislative position was strengthened, while German leader
      <a href="/wiki/Angela_Merkel" title="Angela Merkel">
       Angela Merkel
      </a>
      "has appeared to be floundering and been badly let down by her advisers in recent months", one commentator said. As such, "serious discord between French and German monetary decision-makers was [comparable to that of] ... 1992–93, at the height of the crisis over the
      <a href="/wiki/European_Monetary_System" title="European Monetary System">
       European Monetary System
      </a>
      , the forerunner to EMU" (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Monetary_Union" title="European Monetary Union">
       European Monetary Union
      </a>
      ). "[H]itherto relatively dormant signs of
      <a href="/wiki/Euroscepticism" title="Euroscepticism">
       euro skepticism
      </a>
      in German public opinion and throughout industry have been multiplying in recent months, making Hollande's proposals increasingly unpalatable to a broad swathe of German opinion. Although considerable controversy will continue to swirl over Greece and Spain, the real battle lines over the future of the euro will be drawn up between Germany and France", the commentary concluded.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Another historical parallel – to 1931 when Germany was burdened with debt, unemployment and austerity while France and the United States were relatively strong creditors –
      <a href="/wiki/Liaquat_Ahamed#Lords_of_Finance" title="Liaquat Ahamed">
       gained attention
      </a>
      in summer 2012
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
      even as Germany received a
      <a href="/wiki/Bond_credit_rating#Credit_rating_agencies" title="Bond credit rating">
       debt-rating
      </a>
      warning of its own.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Direct_and_indirect_usage">
       Direct and indirect usage
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">

       </span>

        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Further information:
      <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">

      <a href="/wiki/International_status_and_usage_of_the_euro" title="International status and usage of the euro">

      , and

      </a>
     </div>

       </a>

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Direct_usage">
       Direct usage
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Direct usage">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The euro is the sole currency of 19
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Union_member_states" title="European Union member states">
       EU member states
      </a>
      : Austria, Belgium, Cyprus, Estonia, Finland, France, Germany, Greece, Ireland, Italy, Latvia, Lithuania, Luxembourg, Malta, the Netherlands, Portugal, Slovakia, Slovenia, and Spain. These countries constitute the "
      <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">
       eurozone
      </a>
      ", some 332 million people in total as of 2013
      <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
       <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit">
        [update]
       </a>
      </sup>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2013_data_sheet_68-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-2013_data_sheet-68">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      With all but two of the remaining EU members obliged to join, together with future members of the EU, the
      <a href="/wiki/Enlargement_of_the_eurozone" title="Enlargement of the eurozone">
       enlargement of the eurozone
      </a>
      is set to continue. Outside the EU, the euro is also the sole currency of Montenegro and Kosovo and several
      <a href="/wiki/Microstates_and_the_European_Union" title="Microstates and the European Union">
       European microstates
      </a>
      (Andorra, Monaco, San Marino and the Vatican City) as well as in four
      <a href="/wiki/Special_member_state_territories_and_the_European_Union" title="Special member state territories and the European Union">
       overseas territories of EU members
      </a>
      that are not themselves part of the EU (
      <a href="/wiki/Saint_Barth%C3%A9lemy" title="Saint Barthélemy">
       Saint Barthélemy
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Saint_Pierre_and_Miquelon" title="Saint Pierre and Miquelon">
       Saint Pierre and Miquelon
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/French_Southern_and_Antarctic_Lands" title="French Southern and Antarctic Lands">
       French Southern and Antarctic Lands
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Akrotiri_and_Dhekelia" title="Akrotiri and Dhekelia">
       Akrotiri and Dhekelia
      </a>
      ). Together this direct usage of the euro outside the EU affects nearly 3 million people.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Euro has been used as a trading currency in Cuba since 1998,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and Syria since 2006.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
      There are also various currencies pegged to the euro (see below). In 2009, Zimbabwe abandoned its
      <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwean_dollar" title="Zimbabwean dollar">
       local currency
      </a>
      and used major currencies instead, including the euro and the United States dollar.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Use_as_reserve_currency">
       Use as reserve currency
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Use as reserve currency">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Since its introduction, the euro has been the second most widely held international
      <a href="/wiki/Reserve_currency" title="Reserve currency">
       reserve currency
      </a>
      after the U.S. dollar. The share of the euro as a reserve currency has increased from 18% in 1999 to 27% in 2008. Over this period the share of the U.S. dollar fell from 71% to 64% and the Yen fell from 6.4% to 3.3%. The euro inherited and built on the status of the
      <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Mark" title="Deutsche Mark">
       Deutsche Mark
      </a>
      as the second most important reserve currency. The euro remains underweight as a reserve currency in advanced economies while overweight in emerging and developing economies: according to the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">
       International Monetary Fund
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-external1_72-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-external1-72">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
      the total of euro held as a reserve in the world at the end of 2008 was equal to $1.1 trillion or €850 billion, with a share of 22% of all currency reserves in advanced economies, but a total of 31% of all currency reserves in emerging and developing economies.
     </p>
     <p>
      The possibility of the euro becoming the first international reserve currency is now widely debated among economists.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Former
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Federal_Reserve" title="US Federal Reserve">
       US Federal Reserve
      </a>
      Chairman
      <a href="/wiki/Alan_Greenspan" title="Alan Greenspan">
       Alan Greenspan
      </a>
      gave his opinion in September 2007 that it is "absolutely conceivable that the euro will replace the US dollar as reserve currency, or will be traded as an equally important reserve currency".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In contrast to Greenspan's 2007 assessment, the euro's increase in the share of the worldwide currency reserve basket has slowed considerably since 2007 and since the beginning of the worldwide
      <a href="/wiki/Credit_crunch" title="Credit crunch">
       credit crunch
      </a>
      related recession and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_sovereign-debt_crisis" title="European sovereign-debt crisis">
       European sovereign-debt crisis
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-external1_72-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-external1-72">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Currencies_pegged_to_the_euro">
       Currencies pegged to the euro
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Currencies pegged to the euro">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/International_status_and_usage_of_the_euro" title="International status and usage of the euro">
       International status and usage of the euro
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DOLLAR_AND_EURO_IN_THE_WORLD.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="863" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/DOLLAR_AND_EURO_IN_THE_WORLD.svg/300px-DOLLAR_AND_EURO_IN_THE_WORLD.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/DOLLAR_AND_EURO_IN_THE_WORLD.svg/450px-DOLLAR_AND_EURO_IN_THE_WORLD.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/DOLLAR_AND_EURO_IN_THE_WORLD.svg/600px-DOLLAR_AND_EURO_IN_THE_WORLD.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DOLLAR_AND_EURO_IN_THE_WORLD.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Worldwide use of the euro and the US dollar:
        <div class="legend">
         <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #092D98; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
         </span>
         <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">
          Eurozone
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="legend">
         <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #98b3ff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
         </span>
         External adopters of the euro
        </div>
        <div class="legend">
         <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #510999; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
         </span>
         Currencies pegged to the euro
        </div>
        <div class="legend">
         <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #CC99FF; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
         </span>
         Currencies pegged to the euro within narrow band
        </div>
        <div class="legend">
         <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #099811; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
         </span>
         United States
        </div>
        <div class="legend">
         <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #99FF9E; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
         </span>
         External adopters of the US dollar
        </div>
        <div class="legend">
         <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #999909; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
         </span>
         Currencies pegged to the US dollar
        </div>
        <div class="legend">
         <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FFFF99; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
         </span>
         Currencies pegged to the US dollar within narrow band
        </div>
        <hr/>
        <small>
         Note: The
         <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_ruble" title="Belarusian ruble">
          Belarusian ruble
         </a>
         is pegged to the Euro,
         <a href="/wiki/Russian_ruble" title="Russian ruble">
          Russian ruble
         </a>
         and US$ in a currency basket.
        </small>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Outside the eurozone, a total of 22 countries and territories that do not belong to the EU have currencies that are directly pegged to the euro including 13 countries in mainland Africa (
      <a href="/wiki/CFA_franc" title="CFA franc">
       CFA franc
      </a>
      ), two African island countries (
      <a href="/wiki/Comorian_franc" title="Comorian franc">
       Comorian franc
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cape_Verdean_escudo" title="Cape Verdean escudo">
       Cape Verdean escudo
      </a>
      ), three French Pacific territories (
      <a href="/wiki/CFP_franc" title="CFP franc">
       CFP franc
      </a>
      ) and three Balkan countries, Bosnia and Herzegovina (
      <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_convertible_mark" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina convertible mark">
       Bosnia and Herzegovina convertible mark
      </a>
      ), Bulgaria (
      <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_lev" title="Bulgarian lev">
       Bulgarian lev
      </a>
      ) and Macedonia (
      <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_denar" title="Macedonian denar">
       Macedonian denar
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
      On 28 July 2009, São Tomé and Príncipe signed an agreement with Portugal which will eventually tie its currency to the euro.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Additionally, the
      <a href="/wiki/Moroccan_dirham" title="Moroccan dirham">
       Moroccan dirham
      </a>
      is tied to a basket of currencies, including the Euro and the US dollar, with the Euro given the highest weighting.
     </p>
     <p>
      With the exception of Bosnia, Bulgaria, Macedonia (which had pegged their currencies against the Deutsche Mark) and Cape Verde (formerly pegged to the Portuguese escudo), all of these non-EU countries had a currency peg to the French Franc before pegging their currencies to the euro. Pegging a country's currency to a major currency is regarded as a safety measure, especially for currencies of areas with weak economies, as the euro is seen as a stable currency, prevents runaway inflation and encourages foreign investment due to its stability.
     </p>
     <p>
      Within the EU several currencies have a peg to the euro, in most instances as a precondition to joining the eurozone. The
      <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_lev" title="Bulgarian lev">
       Bulgarian lev
      </a>
      was formerly pegged to the Deutsche Mark; one other EU member state has a direct peg due to ERM II: the
      <a href="/wiki/Danish_krone" title="Danish krone">
       Danish krone
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In total, as of 2013
      <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
       <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit">
        [update]
       </a>
      </sup>
      , 182 million people in Africa use a currency pegged to the euro, 27 million people outside the eurozone in Europe, and another 545,000 people on Pacific islands.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2013_data_sheet_68-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-2013_data_sheet-68">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Since 2005, stamps issued by the
      <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_Military_Order_of_Malta" title="Sovereign Military Order of Malta">
       Sovereign Military Order of Malta
      </a>
      have been denominated in euros, although the Order's official currency remains the
      <a href="/wiki/Maltese_scudo" title="Maltese scudo">
       Maltese scudo
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smom_77-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-smom-77">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Maltese scudo itself is pegged to the euro and is only recognised as legal tender within the Order.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">
       Economics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Economics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Optimal_currency_area">
       Optimal currency area
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Optimal currency area">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Further information:
      <a href="/wiki/Optimum_currency_area" title="Optimum currency area">
       Optimum currency area
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      In economics, an optimum currency area, or region (OCA or OCR), is a geographical region in which it would maximise economic efficiency to have the entire region share a single currency. There are two models, both proposed by
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mundell" title="Robert Mundell">
       Robert Mundell
      </a>
      : the
      <a href="/wiki/Optimum_currency_area#OCA_with_stationary_expectations" title="Optimum currency area">
       stationary expectations model
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Optimum_currency_area#OCA_with_international_risk_sharing" title="Optimum currency area">
       international risk sharing model
      </a>
      . Mundell himself advocates the international risk sharing model and thus concludes in favour of the euro.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, even before the creation of the single currency, there were concerns over diverging economies. Before the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late-2000s_recession" title="Late-2000s recession">
       late-2000s recession
      </a>
      the chances of a state leaving the euro, or the chances that the whole zone would collapse, were considered extremely slim.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However the
      <a href="/wiki/Greek_government-debt_crisis" title="Greek government-debt crisis">
       Greek government-debt crisis
      </a>
      led to former
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Foreign_Secretary" title="British Foreign Secretary">
       British Foreign Secretary
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Jack_Straw" title="Jack Straw">
       Jack Straw
      </a>
      claiming the eurozone could not last in its current form.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Part of the problem seems to be the rules that were created when the euro was set up. John Lanchester, writing for
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">
        The New Yorker
       </a>
      </i>
      , explains it thus:
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       The guiding principle of the currency, which opened for business in 1999, were supposed to be a set of rules to limit a country's annual deficit to three per cent of gross domestic product, and the total accumulated debt to sixty per cent of G.D.P. It was a nice idea, but by 2004 the two biggest economies in the euro zone, Germany and France, had broken the rules for three years in a row.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <h3>

        ]
       </span>
      </span>

        Other
       </td>

        7.1%
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td colspan="3" style="text-align:right;">
        <b>

        </b>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
         <a href="#cite_note-84">
          [note 21]
         </a>
