       <td style="text-align:right;">
        <b>
         200.0%
        </b>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      The most obvious benefit of adopting a single currency is to remove the cost of exchanging currency, theoretically allowing businesses and individuals to consummate previously unprofitable trades. For consumers, banks in the eurozone must charge the same for intra-member cross-border transactions as purely domestic transactions for electronic payments (e.g.,
      <a href="/wiki/Credit_card" title="Credit card">
       credit cards
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Debit_card" title="Debit card">
       debit cards
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cash_machine" title="Cash machine">
       cash machine
      </a>
      withdrawals).
     </p>
     <p>
      The absence of distinct currencies also theoretically removes
      <a href="/wiki/Exchange_rate" title="Exchange rate">
       exchange rate
      </a>
      risks, although the imposition of transfer restrictions in
      <a href="/wiki/2012%E2%80%9313_Cypriot_financial_crisis" title="2012–13 Cypriot financial crisis">
       2012–13 Cypriot financial crisis
      </a>
      means that the situation is not quite so simple. The risk of unanticipated exchange rate movement has always added an additional risk or uncertainty for companies or individuals that invest or trade outside their own currency zones. Companies that
      <a href="/wiki/Hedge_(finance)" title="Hedge (finance)">
       hedge
      </a>
      against this risk will no longer need to shoulder this additional cost. This is particularly important for countries whose currencies had traditionally fluctuated a great deal, particularly the Mediterranean nations
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Financial markets on the continent are expected to be far more
      <a href="/wiki/Market_liquidity" title="Market liquidity">
       liquid
      </a>
      and flexible than they were in the past. The reduction in cross-border transaction costs will allow larger banking firms to provide a wider array of banking services that can compete across and beyond the eurozone. However, although transaction costs were reduced, some studies have shown that
      <a href="/wiki/Risk_aversion" title="Risk aversion">
       risk aversion
      </a>
      has increased during the last 40 years in the Eurozone.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Price_parity">
       Price parity
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Price parity">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Another effect of the common European currency is that differences in prices—in particular in price levels—should decrease because of the
      <a href="/wiki/Law_of_one_price" title="Law of one price">
       law of one price
      </a>
      . Differences in prices can trigger
      <a href="/wiki/Arbitrage" title="Arbitrage">
       arbitrage
      </a>
      , i.e.,
      <a href="/wiki/Speculation" title="Speculation">
       speculative
      </a>
      trade in a
      <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">
       commodity
      </a>
      across borders purely to exploit the price differential. Therefore, prices on commonly traded goods are likely to converge, causing inflation in some regions and deflation in others during the transition. Some evidence of this has been observed in specific eurozone markets.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Macroeconomic_stability">
       Macroeconomic stability
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Macroeconomic stability">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Low levels of inflation are the hallmark of stable and modern economies. Because a high level of inflation acts as a tax (
      <a href="/wiki/Seigniorage" title="Seigniorage">
       seigniorage
      </a>
      ) and theoretically discourages investment, it is generally viewed as undesirable. In spite of the downside, many countries have been unable or unwilling to deal with serious inflationary pressures. Some countries have successfully contained them by establishing largely independent central banks. One such bank was the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bundesbank" title="Bundesbank">
       Bundesbank
      </a>
      in Germany; as the European Central Bank is modelled on the Bundesbank,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bundesbank_87-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bundesbank-87">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
      it is independent of the pressures of national governments and has a mandate to keep inflation low. Member countries that join the euro hope to enjoy the macroeconomic stability associated with low levels of inflation. The ECB (unlike the
      <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_System" title="Federal Reserve System">
       Federal Reserve
      </a>
      in the United States of America) does not have a second objective to sustain growth and employment.
     </p>
     <p>
      Many national and corporate
      <a href="/wiki/Bond_(finance)" title="Bond (finance)">
       bonds
      </a>
      denominated in euro are significantly more liquid and have lower interest rates than was historically the case when denominated in national currencies. While increased liquidity may lower the
      <a href="/wiki/Nominal_interest_rate" title="Nominal interest rate">
       nominal interest rate
      </a>
      on the bond, denominating the bond in a currency with low levels of inflation arguably plays a much larger role. A credible commitment to low levels of inflation and a stable debt reduces the risk that the value of the debt will be eroded by higher levels of inflation or default in the future, allowing debt to be issued at a lower nominal interest rate.
     </p>
     <p>
      Unfortunately, there is also a cost in structurally keeping inflation lower than in the United States, UK, and China. The result is that seen from those countries, the euro has become expensive, making European products increasingly expensive for its largest importers. Hence export from the euro zone becomes more difficult. This is one of the main reasons why economic growth inside the euro zone now lags behind growth in other large economies.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      This effect is strongest in European countries with a weak economy.
     </p>
     <p>
      In general, those in Europe who own large amounts of euros are served by high stability and low inflation. Those who now need to earn euros, including those countries who need to pay interest on large debts, are likely better served with a slightly less strong euro leading to more export. Because with a lower euro, investors would see better chances for (companies in) southern European countries to grow themselves out of the crisis. As a result, investing there would become less risky, and that would push interest rates for southern countries more in line with the European average.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The contradiction here is that high macroeconomic stability in the form of ongoing historically low inflation over time leads to economic problems, creating higher interest rates and political and economic instability for the weaker partners.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Trade">
       Trade
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Trade">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      A 2009 consensus from the studies of the introduction of the euro concluded that it has increased trade within the eurozone by 5% to 10%,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
      although one study suggested an increase of only 3%
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        [68]
       </a>
      </sup>
      while another estimated 9 to 14%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, a
      <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">
       meta-analysis
      </a>
      of all available studies suggests that the prevalence of positive estimates is caused by
      <a href="/wiki/Publication_bias" title="Publication bias">
       publication bias
      </a>
      and that the underlying effect may be negligible.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        [70]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Furthermore, studies accounting for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Time_trend_analysis" title="Time trend analysis">
       time trend
      </a>
      reflecting general cohesion policies in Europe that started before, and continue after implementing the common currency find no effect on trade.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
      These results suggest that other policies aimed at European integration might be the source of observed increase in trade.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Investment">
       Investment
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Investment">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Physical investment seems to have increased by 5% in the eurozone due to the introduction.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        [73]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Regarding foreign direct investment, a study found that the intra-eurozone FDI stocks have increased by about 20% during the first four years of the EMU.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        [74]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Concerning the effect on corporate investment, there is evidence that the introduction of the euro has resulted in an increase in investment rates and that it has made it easier for firms to access financing in Europe. The euro has most specifically stimulated investment in companies that come from countries that previously had weak currencies. A study found that the introduction of the euro accounts for 22% of the investment rate after 1998 in countries that previously had a weak currency.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        [75]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Inflation">
       Inflation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Inflation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The introduction of the euro has led to extensive discussion about its possible effect on inflation. In the short term, there was a widespread impression in the population of the eurozone that the introduction of the euro had led to an increase in prices, but this impression was not confirmed by general indices of inflation and other studies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
       <a href="#cite_note-97">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
       <a href="#cite_note-98">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A study of this paradox found that this was due to an asymmetric effect of the introduction of the euro on prices: while it had no effect on most goods, it had an effect on cheap goods which have seen their price round up after the introduction of the euro. The study found that consumers based their beliefs on inflation of those cheap goods which are frequently purchased.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
       <a href="#cite_note-99">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It has also been suggested that the jump in small prices may be because prior to the introduction, retailers made fewer upward adjustments and waited for the introduction of the euro to do so.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
       <a href="#cite_note-100">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Exchange_rate_risk">
       Exchange rate risk
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Exchange rate risk">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      One of the advantages of the adoption of a common currency is the reduction of the risk associated with changes in currency exchange rates. It has been found that the introduction of the euro created "significant reductions in market risk exposures for nonfinancial firms both in and outside of Europe".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
       <a href="#cite_note-101">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      These reductions in market risk "were concentrated in firms domiciled in the eurozone and in non-Euro firms with a high fraction of foreign sales or assets in Europe".
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Financial_integration">
       Financial integration
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Financial integration">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The introduction of the euro seems to have had a strong effect on European financial integration. According to a study on this question, it has "significantly reshaped the European financial system, especially with respect to the securities markets [...] However, the real and policy barriers to integration in the retail and corporate banking sectors remain significant, even if the wholesale end of banking has been largely integrated."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Specifically, the euro has significantly decreased the cost of trade in bonds, equity, and banking assets within the eurozone.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
       <a href="#cite_note-103">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
      On a global level, there is evidence that the introduction of the euro has led to an integration in terms of investment in bond portfolios, with eurozone countries lending and borrowing more between each other than with other countries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
       <a href="#cite_note-104">
        [83]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_on_interest_rates">
       Effect on interest rates
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Effect on interest rates">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Long-term_interest_rates_of_eurozone_countries_since_1993.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="634" data-file-width="1264" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Long-term_interest_rates_of_eurozone_countries_since_1993.png/300px-Long-term_interest_rates_of_eurozone_countries_since_1993.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Long-term_interest_rates_of_eurozone_countries_since_1993.png/450px-Long-term_interest_rates_of_eurozone_countries_since_1993.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Long-term_interest_rates_of_eurozone_countries_since_1993.png/600px-Long-term_interest_rates_of_eurozone_countries_since_1993.png 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Long-term_interest_rates_of_eurozone_countries_since_1993.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Long-term interest rates of Euro countries, 1993–2016
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      As of January 2014, and since the introduction of the euro, interest rates of most members countries (particularly those with a weak currency), have decreased. The countries whose interest rates fell most as a result of the adoption of the euro are Greece, Ireland, Portugal, Spain, and Italy. These very countries have had the most serious sovereign financing problems.
     </p>
     <p>
      The effect of declining interest rates, combined with excess liquidity continually provided by the ECB, made it easier for banks within the countries in which interest rates fell the most, and their linked sovereigns, to borrow significant amounts (above the 3% of GDP budget deficit imposed on the eurozone initially) and significantly inflate their public and private debt levels.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
       <a href="#cite_note-105">
        [84]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Following the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late-2000s_financial_crisis" title="Late-2000s financial crisis">
       late-2000s financial crisis
      </a>
      , governments in these countries found it necessary to bail out or nationalise their privately held banks to prevent systemic failure of the banking system when underlying hard or financial asset values were found to be grossly inflated and sometimes so near worthless there was no liquid market for them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
       <a href="#cite_note-106">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This further increased the already high levels of public debt to a level the markets began to consider unsustainable, via increasing government bond interest rates, producing the ongoing European sovereign-debt crisis.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Price_convergence">
       Price convergence
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Price convergence">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The evidence on the convergence of prices in the eurozone with the introduction of the euro is mixed. Several studies failed to find any evidence of convergence following the introduction of the euro after a phase of convergence in the early 1990s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
       <a href="#cite_note-107">
        [86]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
       <a href="#cite_note-108">
        [87]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other studies have found evidence of price convergence,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
       <a href="#cite_note-109">
        [88]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
       <a href="#cite_note-110">
        [89]
       </a>
      </sup>
      in particular for cars.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
       <a href="#cite_note-111">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A possible reason for the divergence between the different studies is that the processes of convergence may not have been linear, slowing down substantially between 2000 and 2003, and resurfacing after 2003 as suggested by a recent study (2009).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
       <a href="#cite_note-112">
        [91]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">
       Tourism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Tourism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      A study suggests that the introduction of the euro has had a positive effect on the amount of tourist travel within the EMU, with an increase of 6.5%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
       <a href="#cite_note-113">
        [92]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Exchange_rates">
       Exchange rates
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Exchange rates">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EUR-USD.PNG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="599" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/EUR-USD.PNG/300px-EUR-USD.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/EUR-USD.PNG/450px-EUR-USD.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c5/EUR-USD.PNG 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EUR-USD.PNG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Euro-US Dollar exchange rate, starting from 2002. More recent information is available
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.ecb.europa.eu/stats/exchange/eurofxref/html/eurofxref-graph-usd.en.html" rel="nofollow">
         here
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Flexible_exchange_rates">
       Flexible exchange rates
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Flexible exchange rates">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The ECB targets
      <a href="/wiki/Interest_rate" title="Interest rate">
       interest rates
      </a>
      rather than
      <a href="/wiki/Exchange_rate" title="Exchange rate">
       exchange rates
      </a>
      and in general does not intervene on the foreign exchange rate markets. This is because of the implications of the
      <a href="/wiki/Mundell%E2%80%93Fleming_model" title="Mundell–Fleming model">
       Mundell–Fleming model
      </a>
      , which implies a central bank cannot (without
      <a href="/wiki/Capital_control" title="Capital control">
       capital controls
      </a>
      ) maintain interest rate and exchange rate targets simultaneously, because increasing the
      <a href="/wiki/Money_supply" title="Money supply">
       money supply
      </a>
      results in a
      <a href="/wiki/Depreciation" title="Depreciation">
       depreciation
      </a>
      of the currency. In the years following the
      <a href="/wiki/Single_European_Act" title="Single European Act">
       Single European Act
      </a>
      , the EU has liberalised its capital markets, and as the ECB has chosen monetary autonomy, the exchange-rate regime of the euro is flexible, or
      <a href="/wiki/Floating_exchange_rate" title="Floating exchange rate">
       floating
      </a>
      . The result of the ECB maintaining historically low interest rates and restricting money supply has been that over the last decade the euro has become expensive relative to the currency of Europe's main trading partners. However, in 2010, the euro started on a sharp decline. Starting at U.S$1.60 in 2008, and dropping to
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">
       US$
      </a>
      1.04 in 2015. The
      <a href="/wiki/Canadian_dollar" title="Canadian dollar">
       Canadian dollar
      </a>
      despite seeing a decline in value against the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USD" title="USD">
       USD
      </a>
      , has seen a growth in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EURO" title="EURO">
       EURO
      </a>
      -&gt; [CAD].
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Against_other_major_currencies">
       Against other major currencies
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Against other major currencies">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The euro is the second-most widely held
      <a href="/wiki/Reserve_currency" title="Reserve currency">
       reserve currency
      </a>
      after the U.S. dollar. After its introduction on 4 January 1999 its exchange rate against the other major currencies fell reaching its lowest exchange rates in 2000 (25 October vs the U.S. dollar, 26 October vs Japanese Yen, 3 May vs Pound Sterling). Afterwards it regained and its exchange rate reached its historical highest point in 2008 (15 July vs U.S. dollar, 23 July vs Japanese Yen, 29 December vs Pound Sterling). With the advent of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late-2000s_financial_crisis" title="Late-2000s financial crisis">
       global financial crisis
      </a>
      the euro initially fell, only to regain later. Despite pressure due to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_sovereign-debt_crisis" title="European sovereign-debt crisis">
       European sovereign-debt crisis
      </a>
      the euro remained stable.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
       <a href="#cite_note-114">
        [93]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In November 2011 the euro's exchange rate index – measured against currencies of the bloc's major trading partners – was trading almost two percent higher on the year, approximately at the same level as it was before the crisis kicked off in 2007.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
       <a href="#cite_note-115">
        [94]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Current and historical exchange rates against 29 other currencies (European Central Bank)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
        <a href="#cite_note-116">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Current dollar/euro exchange rates (BBC)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
        <a href="#cite_note-117">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Historical exchange rate from 1971 until now
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
        <a href="#cite_note-118">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Linguistic_issues">
       Linguistic issues
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Linguistic issues">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_issues_concerning_the_euro" title="Linguistic issues concerning the euro">
       Linguistic issues concerning the euro
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EUR_5_obverse_(2013_issue).png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="364" data-file-width="709" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/EUR_5_obverse_%282013_issue%29.png/220px-EUR_5_obverse_%282013_issue%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/EUR_5_obverse_%282013_issue%29.png/330px-EUR_5_obverse_%282013_issue%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/EUR_5_obverse_%282013_issue%29.png/440px-EUR_5_obverse_%282013_issue%29.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EUR_5_obverse_(2013_issue).png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/5_euro_note" title="5 euro note">
         5 euro note
        </a>
        from the new Europa series written in
        <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">
         Latin
        </a>
        (EURO) and
        <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">
         Greek
        </a>
        (EYPΩ) alphabets, but also in the
        <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_alphabet" title="Bulgarian alphabet">
         Cyrillic
        </a>
        (EBPO) alphabet, as a result of
        <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">
         Bulgaria
        </a>
        joining the European Union in 2007.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The formal titles of the currency are
      <i>
       euro
      </i>
      for the major unit and
      <i>
       cent
      </i>
      for the minor (one hundredth) unit and for official use in most eurozone languages; according to the ECB, all languages should use the same spelling for the nominative singular.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
       <a href="#cite_note-119">
        [98]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This may contradict normal rules for word formation in some languages, e.g., those where there is no
      <i>
       eu
      </i>
      <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">
       diphthong
      </a>
      . Bulgaria has negotiated an exception;
      <i>
       euro
      </i>
      in the Bulgarian Cyrillic alphabet is spelled as eвро (
      <i>
       evro
      </i>
      ) and not eуро (
      <i>
       euro
      </i>
      ) in all official documents.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
       <a href="#cite_note-120">
        [99]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the Greek script the term ευρώ (evró) is used; the Greek "cent" coins are denominated in λεπτό/ά (leptó/á). Official practice for English-language EU legislation is to use the words euro and cent as both singular and plural,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
       <a href="#cite_note-121">
        [100]
       </a>
      </sup>
      although the European Commission's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Directorate-General_for_Translation_(European_Commission)" title="Directorate-General for Translation (European Commission)">
       Directorate-General for Translation
      </a>
      states that the plural forms
      <i>
       euros
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       cents
      </i>
      should be used in English.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
       <a href="#cite_note-122">
        [101]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">
       Criticism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Criticism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Unemployment">
       Unemployment
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Unemployment">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Nobel memorial prize-winning economist
      <a href="/wiki/James_Meade" title="James Meade">
       James Meade
      </a>
      thought that a central bank should not make price stability the objective of aggregate demand management. When prices are likely to be pushed up due to increase of indirect taxes or adverse terms-of-trade shocks, the surge in prices must be cancelled out by decline in domestic money wage costs, as long as such a price stability policy is adopted.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meade1978ngdp_123-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-meade1978ngdp-123">
        [102]
       </a>
      </sup>
      There would be unemployment in the all industrial sectors under a price stabilisation policy, suggesting that in the short run the elasticity of demand for labour is low.
     </p>
     <p>
      Under the ECB's price stabilisation policy, many people in the eurozone have difficulty finding a job. The unemployment rate of Spain is around 25 percent in 2014, and an economic forecast says that the figure will not decrease below 20 percent until 2017.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
       <a href="#cite_note-124">
        [103]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      ELSTAT, the statistics agency of Greece, shows that Greece's unemployment rate was 27 percent in June 2014.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
       <a href="#cite_note-125">
        [104]
       </a>
      </sup>
      OECD forecasts that Greece's unemployment rate will remain around 27 percent until 2016. Due to long-term unemployment, skills of jobless persons have been depreciated and their motivation of finding jobs has been lost, which causes the country's level of unemployment to remain high.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
       <a href="#cite_note-126">
        [105]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="chart" style="margin-top:1em;max-width:900px;">
      <div style="position:relative;min-height:350px;min-width:900px;max-width:900px;">

        </div>
       </div>
       <div style="position:absolute;top:270px;left:160px;width:740px;">
        <div style="position:absolute;left:5px;top:10px;min-width:82px;max-width:82px;text-align:center;veritical-align:top;">
         Portugal
        </div>
        <div style="position:absolute;left:46px;height:10px;width:1px;border-left:1px solid black;">
        </div>
        <div style="position:absolute;left:97px;top:10px;min-width:82px;max-width:82px;text-align:center;veritical-align:top;">
         Spain
        </div>
        <div style="position:absolute;left:138px;height:10px;width:1px;border-left:1px solid black;">
        </div>
        <div style="position:absolute;left:189px;top:10px;min-width:82px;max-width:82px;text-align:center;veritical-align:top;">
         Italy
        </div>
        <div style="position:absolute;left:230px;height:10px;width:1px;border-left:1px solid black;">
        </div>
        <div style="position:absolute;left:281px;top:10px;min-width:82px;max-width:82px;text-align:center;veritical-align:top;">
         Greece
        </div>
        <div style="position:absolute;left:322px;height:10px;width:1px;border-left:1px solid black;">
        </div>
        <div style="position:absolute;left:373px;top:10px;min-width:82px;max-width:82px;text-align:center;veritical-align:top;">
         EuroZone (average)
        </div>
        <div style="position:absolute;left:414px;height:10px;width:1px;border-left:1px solid black;">
        </div>
        <div style="position:absolute;left:465px;top:10px;min-width:82px;max-width:82px;text-align:center;veritical-align:top;">
         Finland
        </div>
        <div style="position:absolute;left:506px;height:10px;width:1px;border-left:1px solid black;">
        </div>
        <div style="position:absolute;left:557px;top:10px;min-width:82px;max-width:82px;text-align:center;veritical-align:top;">
         Ireland
        </div>
        <div style="position:absolute;left:598px;height:10px;width:1px;border-left:1px solid black;">
        </div>
        <div style="position:absolute;left:649px;top:10px;min-width:82px;max-width:82px;text-align:center;veritical-align:top;">
         Germany
        </div>
        <div style="position:absolute;left:690px;height:10px;width:1px;border-left:1px solid black;">
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div>
       <ul style="width:100%;list-style:none;-webkit-column-width:12em;-moz-column-width:12em;column-width:12em;">
        <li>
         <span style="padding:0 1em;background-color:grey;box-shadow:2px -1px 4px 0 silver;margin-right:1em;">
         </span>
         jobless
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IFS2014augIMFU_127-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-IFS2014augIMFU-127">
           [106]
          </a>
         </sup>
         persons(thousands)4Q 2013
        </li>
        <li>
         <span style="padding:0 1em;background-color:red;box-shadow:2px -1px 4px 0 silver;margin-right:1em;">
         </span>
         unemployment rate(%)4Q 2013
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IFS2014augIMFU_127-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-IFS2014augIMFU-127">
           [106]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </li>
       </ul>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Spain's youth unemployment rate is 53.8 percent in July 2014, and this is the highest figure in the eurozone.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ffi2014aug29U_128-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ffi2014aug29U-128">
        [107]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This figure is comparable to 53.1 percent of the Greek youth unemployment in May 2014.
     </p>
     <p>
      In July 2014, the averaged unemployment rate of the eurozone is 11.7 percent, slight decrease from 11.9 percent in 2013.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IFS2014augIMFU_127-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-IFS2014augIMFU-127">
        [106]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ffi2014aug29U_128-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ffi2014aug29U-128">
        [107]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Likewise,
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_Krugman" title="Paul Krugman">
       Paul Krugman
      </a>
      argued that the existence of a single shared currency across the entire eurozone, in combination with tight money policies of the ECB (motivated by insistence of Germany on low inflation), placed much of Southern Europe in a state of permanent high unemployment. According to Krugman, during the period between the creation of the euro and the 2008 financial crisis, countries of Southern Europe experienced abnormally high rates of wage growth due to high influx of investor money. Between 2000 and 2008, unit labor costs actually declined slightly in Germany, but rose by 30% in Spain and Greece.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129">
       <a href="#cite_note-129">
        [108]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This created an imbalance that put these countries at competitive disadvantage relative to Northern Europe. Returning to full employment at this point requires that the labor costs gap is somehow cancelled. If Spain and Greece had their own currencies, this would have easily happened through exchange rate adjustment. Since they don't, it can either happen through a decrease in nominal wages in Spain and Greece, also known as "internal devaluation", (an extremely difficult and slow process, since nominal wages, in general, exhibit
      <a href="/wiki/Nominal_rigidity" title="Nominal rigidity">
       downward rigidity
      </a>
      ), or through an equal increase in nominal wages (i.e. inflation) in Northern Europe, which does not happen because of active resistance from Germany.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
       <a href="#cite_note-130">
        [109]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">
       <a href="#cite_note-131">
        [110]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

       <li id="cite_note-34">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-34">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <cite class="citation web">
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.ecb.int/ecb/orga/capital/html/index.en.html" rel="nofollow">
           "Capital Subscription"
          </a>
          . European Central Bank
          <span class="reference-accessdate">
           . Retrieved
           <span class="nowrap">
            18 December
           </span>
           2011
          </span>
          .
          <q>
           The NCBs' shares in this capital are calculated using a key which reflects the respective country's share in the total population and gross domestic product of the EU – in equal weightings. The ECB adjusts the shares every five years and whenever a new country joins the EU. The adjustment is done on the basis of data provided by the European Commission.
          </q>

       <li id="cite_note-119">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-119">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <cite class="citation web">
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.ecb.int/pub/pdf/conrep/cr200705en.pdf" rel="nofollow">
           "European Central Bank, Covergence report May 2007"
          </a>
          <span style="font-size:85%;">
           (PDF)
          </span>
          <span class="reference-accessdate">
           . Retrieved
           <span class="nowrap">
            29 December
           </span>
           2008
          </span>
          .
          <q>
           The euro is the single currency of the member states that have adopted it. To make this singleness apparent, Community law requires a single spelling of the word
           <i>
            euro
           </i>
           in the nominative singular case in all Community and national legislative provisions, taking into account the existence of different alphabets.
          </q>
         </cite>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEuro&amp;rft.btitle=European+Central+Bank%2C+Covergence+report+May+2007&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.ecb.int%2Fpub%2Fpdf%2Fconrep%2Fcr200705en.pdf&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>

       <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEuro&amp;rft.aufirst=Richard&amp;rft.aulast=Baldwin&amp;rft.au=Wyplosz%2C+Charles&amp;rft.btitle=The+Economics+of+European+Integration&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-07-710394-7&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=McGraw+Hill&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
        <span style="display:none;">
        </span>
       </span>
      </li>

       <cite class="citation book">
